CSS Course

Introduction to CSS

CSS, or Cascading Style Sheets, is a stylesheet language used to control the presentation and layout of HTML documents. While HTML provides the structure of a webpage (like headings, paragraphs, and images), CSS defines how that structure looks—its colors, fonts, sizes, spacing, and more. CSS was first proposed by Håkon Wium Lie in 1994 and officially introduced in 1996 by the World Wide Web Consortium (W3C). Since then, it has evolved into a powerful tool for creating visually appealing, responsive, and interactive web designs.

The "Cascading" in CSS refers to the way styles are applied in a hierarchical manner. Styles can cascade down from parent elements to child elements, be overridden based on specificity, or combine to create the final appearance of an element. This separation of design (CSS) from content (HTML) makes websites easier to maintain, update, and scale. CSS also enables developers to create consistent designs across multiple pages and adapt layouts for different devices, such as desktops, tablets, and smartphones.

Why Learn CSS?

  • Styling: Adds visual appeal to plain HTML with colors, fonts, and layouts.
  • Separation of Concerns: Keeps design separate from structure, improving maintainability.
  • Responsiveness: Adapts designs for various devices (desktops, phones, tablets).
  • Interactivity: Enables animations, transitions, and hover effects for user engagement.
  • Efficiency: Allows styling of multiple pages with a single CSS file.


This is plain text.





This text is styled with CSS!

Example Explanation: The first paragraph is plain HTML with no styling, resulting in a default appearance. The second paragraph uses CSS to change its color to blue, increase the font size, add a light gray background, apply padding, and add a border. This demonstrates how CSS can transform the presentation of HTML elements.

1. What does CSS stand for?

Syntax & Selectors

CSS (Cascading Style Sheets) uses a specific syntax to define how styles are applied to HTML elements. A CSS rule consists of two main parts: a selector and a declaration block. The selector specifies which HTML elements the styles should apply to, while the declaration block, enclosed in curly braces {}, contains one or more declarations. Each declaration is a property-value pair, separated by a colon (:) and ending with a semicolon (;). For example, in the rule p { color: blue; font-size: 16px; }, p is the selector, and color: blue; and font-size: 16px; are declarations.

Selectors are the heart of CSS—they allow you to target specific HTML elements to style. CSS provides a variety of selectors to choose from, including basic ones like element selectors (e.g., h1), class selectors (e.g., .class-name), and ID selectors (e.g., #id-name). You can also combine selectors to target elements more precisely, such as targeting a paragraph with a specific class (p.highlight) or all paragraphs inside a div (div p). Mastering syntax and selectors is crucial for creating well-styled, maintainable web pages.

Key Concepts of CSS Syntax & Selectors

  • Selector: Identifies the HTML element(s) to style (e.g., p, .class, #id).
  • Declaration Block: Contains the styles inside {}, made up of property-value pairs.
  • Property: The style attribute to change (e.g., color, font-size).
  • Value: The specific setting for the property (e.g., blue, 16px).
  • Types of Selectors: Element (e.g., h1), Class (e.g., .intro), ID (e.g., #header), and Combinators (e.g., div p).


This is a Heading


This is an introductory paragraph.


This is a container div.



h1 {
    color: darkblue;
    font-size: 28px;
}

.intro {
    background-color: #e0f7fa;
    padding: 15px;
    font-style: italic;
}

#container {
    border: 2px solid #333;
    width: 50%;
    margin: 20px auto;
    text-align: center;
}

Example 1 Explanation:

  • h1: An element selector targets all <h1> elements, setting their text color to dark blue and font size to 28px.
  • .intro: A class selector targets elements with class="intro", adding a light cyan background, padding, and italic text.
  • #container: An ID selector targets the element with id="container", adding a border, setting the width to 50%, centering it with margin, and aligning the text to the center.




    
This is a regular paragraph.

    
This paragraph is highlighted.





.content p {
    color: #2e7d32;
    font-size: 16px;
}

.content .highlight {
    background-color: #ffeb3b;
    font-weight: bold;
}

Example 2 Explanation:

  • .content p: A descendant selector targets all <p> elements inside an element with class="content", setting their text color to a shade of green and font size to 16px.
  • .content .highlight: Targets only <p> elements with class="highlight" inside .content, adding a yellow background and bold text. This demonstrates how to combine selectors for more specific targeting.

Knowledge Check

1. Which part of the CSS rule .intro { background-color: lightblue; } is the selector?

Types of CSS (Inline, Internal, External)

CSS can be applied to HTML documents in three different ways: Inline CSS, Internal CSS, and External CSS. Each method has its own syntax, use cases, advantages, and drawbacks. Understanding these types is essential for writing efficient and maintainable code, as the method you choose can impact the scalability, reusability, and performance of your web projects.

Choosing the right type of CSS depends on the project's requirements. For small, quick changes, inline CSS might suffice, but for larger projects, external CSS is typically the best choice due to its maintainability and scalability. Let’s explore each type in detail.

1. Inline CSS

Inline CSS is applied directly to an HTML element using the style attribute. The styles are written as a string of CSS property-value pairs within the tag itself. This method is useful for applying unique styles to a single element without affecting others, but it’s not recommended for larger projects due to its lack of reusability and maintainability. 

<!-- Inline CSS Example -->
            <p style="color: red; font-size: 18px; background-color: lightyellow;">This paragraph has inline CSS!</p>

Explanation: In the example above, the <p> tag uses inline CSS to set its text color to red, font size to 18px, and background color to light yellow. The styles are applied directly within the HTML tag using the style attribute.

Advantages of Inline CSS:

  • Quick to apply for small, one-off changes.
  • Doesn’t require a separate CSS file or <style> tag.
  • High specificity—styles applied inline override other styles (unless overridden by !important).

Disadvantages of Inline CSS:

  • Hard to maintain—styles are scattered throughout the HTML, making updates difficult.
  • Not reusable—styles apply only to the specific element they’re written on.
  • Increases HTML file size, which can slow down page loading.
  • Mixes content (HTML) with presentation (CSS), violating the separation of concerns principle.

2. Internal CSS

Internal CSS (also called embedded CSS) is written within a <style> tag in the <head> section of an HTML document. This method allows you to define styles for multiple elements on a single page without affecting other pages. It’s useful for single-page projects or when you want to keep everything in one file, but it’s less efficient for multi-page websites. 

<!-- Internal CSS Example -->
            <!DOCTYPE html>
            <html>
            <head>
                <title>Internal CSS Example</title>
                <style>
                    h1 {
                        color: navy;
                        text-align: center;
                    }
                    p {
                        font-size: 16px;
                        line-height: 1.5;
                        background-color: #f0f0f0;
                        padding: 10px;
                    }
                </style>
            </head>
            <body>
                <h1>Welcome to My Page</h1>
                <p>This paragraph is styled using internal CSS. The styles are defined in the head section.</p>
            </body>
            </html>

Explanation: Here, the <style> tag in the <head> section contains CSS rules for h1 and p elements. The h1 is styled with a navy color and centered text, while all p elements on this page have a font size of 16px, a light gray background, and padding.

Advantages of Internal CSS:

  • Easier to manage than inline CSS since styles are centralized in the <head>.
  • Reusable within the same page—styles can apply to multiple elements.
  • Keeps HTML cleaner compared to inline CSS.

Disadvantages of Internal CSS:

  • Not reusable across multiple pages—styles are limited to the page they’re defined on.
  • Increases the HTML file size, potentially slowing down page load times.
  • Still mixes presentation with content to some extent, though less than inline CSS.

3. External CSS

External CSS is written in a separate .css file and linked to the HTML document using the <link> tag in the <head> section. This is the most recommended method for styling web projects because it promotes reusability, maintainability, and separation of concerns. A single CSS file can be linked to multiple HTML pages, ensuring consistent styling across an entire website. 

<!-- External CSS Example -->
            
            <!-- styles.css -->
            h1 {
                color: green;
                font-family: Arial, sans-serif;
            }
            p {
                font-size: 14px;
                border: 1px solid #ccc;
                padding: 15px;
            }
            
            <!-- index.html -->
            <!DOCTYPE html>
            <html>
            <head>
                <title>External CSS Example</title>
                <link rel="stylesheet" href="styles.css">
            </head>
            <body>
                <h1>This is a Heading</h1>
                <p>This paragraph is styled using external CSS from a separate file.</p>
            </body>
            </html>

Explanation: In this example, the styles are defined in a separate file called styles.css. The HTML file links to this stylesheet using the <link> tag with the rel="stylesheet" and href="styles.css" attributes. The h1 element is styled with a green color and Arial font, while the p element has a border and padding.

Advantages of External CSS:

  • Promotes separation of concerns—HTML (content) and CSS (presentation) are kept in separate files.
  • Reusable across multiple pages—update one CSS file to change the entire site’s styling.
  • Improves performance—browsers can cache the CSS file, reducing load times for subsequent pages.
  • Easier to maintain—centralized styles make updates and debugging simpler.

Disadvantages of External CSS:

  • Requires an additional HTTP request to load the CSS file (unless optimized with techniques like inlining critical CSS).
  • Can lead to complexity in large projects if not organized properly (e.g., using a CSS framework or modular structure can help).

Key Takeaways

  • Inline CSS: Best for quick, one-off styles but not scalable or maintainable.
  • Internal CSS: Good for single-page projects but not reusable across multiple pages.
  • External CSS: The preferred method for most projects due to reusability, maintainability, and performance benefits.
  • Always aim for separation of concerns by using external CSS for larger projects.

Knowledge Check

1. Which type of CSS is best for styling multiple pages consistently?

Colors, Backgrounds, Borders

CSS provides powerful properties to style the visual appearance of HTML elements, including their colors, backgrounds, and borders. These properties allow you to control the text color, background color or image, and the borders surrounding elements, enabling you to create visually appealing and well-structured designs. Understanding how to use these properties effectively is fundamental to web design, as they help define the look and feel of a webpage.

In this section, we’ll explore the key CSS properties for colors, backgrounds, and borders, including how to apply them, the different value formats (e.g., hex, RGB, HSL), and some advanced techniques like gradients and border-radius. Let’s dive into each category with examples.

1. Colors

CSS allows you to set the color of text, backgrounds, borders, and other properties using the color property for text and properties like background-color or border-color for other elements. Colors can be specified in several formats, including:

  • Named Colors: Predefined color names like red, blue, or green.
  • Hexadecimal (Hex): A six-digit code starting with #, e.g., #FF0000 for red.
  • RGB: A function specifying red, green, and blue values, e.g., rgb(255, 0, 0) for red.
  • RGBA: RGB with an alpha channel for opacity, e.g., rgba(255, 0, 0, 0.5) for semi-transparent red.
  • HSL: A function specifying hue, saturation, and lightness, e.g., hsl(0, 100%, 50%) for red.
  • HSLA: HSL with an alpha channel, e.g., hsla(0, 100%, 50%, 0.5).


            
This text is red (named color).

            
This text is blue (hex).

            
This text is green (RGB).

            
This text is purple with opacity (RGBA).

            
This text is orange (HSL).

            
            
            .named {
                color: red;
            }
            .hex {
                color: #0000FF;
            }
            .rgb {
                color: rgb(0, 128, 0);
            }
            .rgba {
                color: rgba(128, 0, 128, 0.7);
            }
            .hsl {
                color: hsl(39, 100%, 50%);
            }

Explanation: The example demonstrates different ways to specify colors for text. Each paragraph uses a different color format:

  • red: A named color for red text.
  • #0000FF: Hex code for blue text.
  • rgb(0, 128, 0): RGB values for green text.
  • rgba(128, 0, 128, 0.7): RGBA for purple text with 70% opacity.
  • hsl(39, 100%, 50%): HSL for orange text (hue of 39 degrees, 100% saturation, 50% lightness).

2. Backgrounds

CSS background properties allow you to style the background of an element with colors, images, gradients, and more. The main background properties include:

  • background-color: Sets a solid color for the background.
  • background-image: Sets an image as the background.
  • background-repeat: Controls how a background image repeats (e.g., repeat, no-repeat, repeat-x, repeat-y).
  • background-position: Positions the background image (e.g., center, top left).
  • background-size: Sets the size of the background image (e.g., cover, contain, or specific dimensions).
  • background: A shorthand property to set multiple background properties at once.
You can also create gradients using linear-gradient() or radial-gradient() as a background image. 


            
Solid color background

            
Background with an image

            
Linear gradient background

            
            
            .bg-color {
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 20px;
                margin-bottom: 10px;
            }
            .bg-image {
                background-image: url('https://via.placeholder.com/150');
                background-repeat: no-repeat;
                background-position: center;
                background-size: cover;
                padding: 50px;
                margin-bottom: 10px;
                color: white;
                text-align: center;
            }
            .bg-gradient {
                background: linear-gradient(to right, #ff7e5f, #feb47b);
                padding: 20px;
                color: white;
            }

Explanation:

  • .bg-color: Uses background-color to set a light cyan background.
  • .bg-image: Sets a background image with background-image, prevents it from repeating with no-repeat, centers it with background-position, and scales it to cover the element with background-size: cover.
  • .bg-gradient: Uses the background shorthand to create a linear gradient from peach (#ff7e5f) to orange (#feb47b), transitioning from left to right.

3. Borders

CSS border properties allow you to add borders around elements, controlling their width, style, and color. The main border properties include:

  • border-width: Sets the thickness of the border (e.g., 2px, thin, medium).
  • border-style: Sets the style of the border (e.g., solid, dashed, dotted, double).
  • border-color: Sets the color of the border.
  • border: A shorthand property to set width, style, and color in one line.
  • border-radius: Rounds the corners of the border (e.g., 10px for all corners, or 10px 20px for different corners).
You can also style individual sides using properties like border-top, border-right, border-bottom, and border-left. 


            
Basic border

            
Dashed border with rounded corners

            
Border on one side only

            
            
            .border-basic {
                border: 2px solid #333;
                padding: 15px;
                margin-bottom: 10px;
            }
            .border-dashed {
                border-width: 3px;
                border-style: dashed;
                border-color: #ff4500;
                border-radius: 10px;
                padding: 15px;
                margin-bottom: 10px;
            }
            .border-side {
                border-left: 5px double #4682b4;
                padding: 15px;
            }

Explanation:

  • .border-basic: Uses the border shorthand to create a 2px solid black border around the element.
  • .border-dashed: Sets a 3px dashed orange border with rounded corners using border-radius.
  • .border-side: Applies a 5px double steel-blue border only to the left side using border-left.

Key Takeaways

  • Use the color property for text and properties like background-color or border-color for other elements, with formats like hex, RGB, or HSL.
  • Background properties like background-image, background-repeat, and background-size give you control over background styling, including gradients.
  • Border properties allow you to customize the width, style, and color of borders, with border-radius for rounded corners and side-specific properties for targeted styling.
  • Combine these properties creatively to enhance the visual design of your webpage.

Knowledge Check

1. Which property would you use to round the corners of a border?

Margins, Padding, Box Model

The CSS Box Model is a fundamental concept that defines how elements are structured and spaced on a webpage. Every HTML element is treated as a rectangular box, consisting of the content area, padding, borders, and margins. Understanding the box model, along with the margin and padding properties, is crucial for creating precise layouts and controlling the spacing between elements.

In this section, we’ll explore the components of the box model, how to use margins and padding to control spacing, and how the box-sizing property affects the box model’s behavior. Let’s break it down with examples.

1. The Box Model

The CSS box model represents the structure of an element as a box with the following layers, from the inside out:

  • Content: The innermost part, containing the element’s text, images, or other content. Its size is determined by the width and height properties.
  • Padding: The space between the content and the border, controlled by the padding property.
  • Border: A layer surrounding the padding, controlled by the border property (e.g., border-width, border-style, border-color).
  • Margin: The outermost layer, representing the space outside the border, controlled by the margin property.
By default, the total width and height of an element include the content, padding, and border (but not the margin). This behavior can be modified using the box-sizing property. 


            
Box Model Example

            
            
            .box-model-example {
                width: 200px;
                height: 100px;
                padding: 20px;
                border: 5px solid #333;
                margin: 30px;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                text-align: center;
                line-height: 100px;
            }

Explanation: In this example:

  • Content: The width is 200px, and the height is 100px.
  • Padding: 20px on all sides, adding 40px to both the total width and height (20px on each side).
  • Border: 5px solid border on all sides, adding 10px to both the total width and height (5px on each side).
  • Margin: 30px on all sides, creating space outside the border (not included in the element’s total size).
  • Total Size (without box-sizing): Width = 200px (content) + 40px (padding) + 10px (border) = 250px; Height = 100px + 40px + 10px = 150px.

The box-sizing property can change this behavior. By default, it’s set to content-box, meaning the width and height only apply to the content. Setting box-sizing: border-box makes the width and height include the padding and border, which is often more intuitive for layout design. 


            
Content-box (default)

            
Border-box

            
            
            .box-sizing-content {
                box-sizing: content-box;
                width: 200px;
                height: 100px;
                padding: 20px;
                border: 5px solid #ff4500;
                margin: 20px;
                background-color: #ffe0b2;
                text-align: center;
                line-height: 100px;
            }
            .box-sizing-border {
                box-sizing: border-box;
                width: 200px;
                height: 100px;
                padding: 20px;
                border: 5px solid #4682b4;
                margin: 20px;
                background-color: #b3e5fc;
                text-align: center;
                line-height: 60px; /* Adjusted due to border-box */
            }

Explanation:

  • .box-sizing-content: Uses box-sizing: content-box. Total width = 200px (content) + 40px (padding) + 10px (border) = 250px.
  • .box-sizing-border: Uses box-sizing: border-box. Total width = 200px (includes content, padding, and border). The content area shrinks to accommodate the padding and border, so the content width is 200px - 40px (padding) - 10px (border) = 150px.

2. Padding

The padding property adds space between an element’s content and its border. It can be set for all sides at once or individually for each side using properties like padding-top, padding-right, padding-bottom, and padding-left. The shorthand padding property accepts 1 to 4 values:

  • 1 value: Applies to all sides (e.g., padding: 10px).
  • 2 values: First value for top/bottom, second for left/right (e.g., padding: 10px 20px).
  • 3 values: First for top, second for left/right, third for bottom (e.g., padding: 10px 20px 30px).
  • 4 values: Top, right, bottom, left (clockwise, e.g., padding: 10px 20px 30px 40px).


            
Padding on all sides

            
Specific padding on each side

            
            
            .padding-all {
                padding: 20px;
                border: 2px solid #333;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                margin: 20px;
                text-align: center;
            }
            .padding-specific {
                padding: 10px 20px 30px 40px;
                border: 2px solid #ff4500;
                background-color: #ffe0b2;
                margin: 20px;
                text-align: center;
            }

Explanation:

  • .padding-all: Applies 20px padding to all sides using a single value.
  • .padding-specific: Uses the 4-value shorthand: 10px (top), 20px (right), 30px (bottom), 40px (left).
Padding increases the space inside the element, pushing the content away from the border. It’s included in the element’s total size unless box-sizing: border-box is used.

3. Margins

The margin property adds space outside an element’s border, creating separation between the element and its neighbors. Like padding, margins can be set for all sides or individually using margin-top, margin-right, margin-bottom, and margin-left. The shorthand margin property follows the same 1-to-4 value syntax as padding. Margins can also be set to auto to center block elements horizontally.

A key concept with margins is margin collapsing: when two vertical margins (e.g., between two elements) overlap, the larger margin is used, and the smaller one is ignored. This only applies to vertical margins, not horizontal ones. 


            
Margin on all sides

            
Centered with margin: auto

            
First element (margin-bottom: 30px)

            
Second element (margin-top: 20px)

            
            
            .margin-all {
                margin: 30px;
                border: 2px solid #333;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 10px;
                text-align: center;
            }
            .margin-center {
                margin: 20px auto;
                width: 200px;
                border: 2px solid #ff4500;
                background-color: #ffe0b2;
                padding: 10px;
                text-align: center;
            }
            .margin-collapse-1 {
                margin-bottom: 30px;
                border: 2px solid #4682b4;
                background-color: #b3e5fc;
                padding: 10px;
                text-align: center;
            }
            .margin-collapse-2 {
                margin-top: 20px;
                border: 2px solid #4682b4;
                background-color: #b3e5fc;
                padding: 10px;
                text-align: center;
            }

Explanation:

  • .margin-all: Applies 30px margin to all sides, creating space around the element.
  • .margin-center: Uses margin: 20px auto to center the element horizontally (requires a defined width).
  • .margin-collapse-1 and .margin-collapse-2: Demonstrate margin collapsing. The first element has a margin-bottom: 30px, and the second has a margin-top: 20px. The vertical space between them will be 30px (the larger margin), not 50px.
Margins are outside the element’s border and are not included in the element’s total size.

Key Takeaways

  • The CSS box model consists of content, padding, border, and margin, with the total size depending on box-sizing (content-box or border-box).
  • padding adds space inside an element, between the content and border, and can be set for all sides or individually.
  • margin adds space outside an element, creating separation from other elements, and supports auto for centering.
  • Vertical margins can collapse, taking the larger value when two margins overlap.

Knowledge Check

1. What does the box-sizing: border-box property do?

Width, Height, Overflow

CSS provides properties to control the dimensions of HTML elements and manage their content when it exceeds those dimensions. The width and height properties define the size of an element’s content area, while the overflow property determines what happens when the content overflows the element’s boundaries. These properties are essential for creating responsive layouts and ensuring content is displayed appropriately.

In this section, we’ll explore how to set the width and height of elements using various units, how to constrain dimensions with min-width, max-width, min-height, and max-height, and how to handle overflow with the overflow property. Let’s dive in with examples.

1. Width and Height

The width and height properties set the dimensions of an element’s content area. These properties can be specified using various units, such as:

  • Pixels (px): Fixed units (e.g., width: 200px).
  • Percentages (%): Relative to the parent element (e.g., width: 50%).
  • Viewport units (vw, vh): Relative to the viewport (e.g., width: 50vw for 50% of the viewport width).
  • Other units: Like rem, em, or auto.
By default, width and height only apply to the content area. If box-sizing: border-box is used, they include padding and border as well. 


            
Fixed size (200px x 100px)

            
Percentage size (50% width)

            
Viewport size (50vw x 30vh)

            
            
            .fixed-size {
                width: 200px;
                height: 100px;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                border: 2px solid #333;
                text-align: center;
                line-height: 100px;
                margin: 20px 0;
            }
            .percentage-size {
                width: 50%;
                height: 80px;
                background-color: #ffe0b2;
                border: 2px solid #ff4500;
                text-align: center;
                line-height: 80px;
                margin: 20px 0;
            }
            .viewport-size {
                width: 50vw;
                height: 30vh;
                background-color: #b3e5fc;
                border: 2px solid #4682b4;
                text-align: center;
                line-height: 30vh;
                margin: 20px 0;
            }

Explanation:

  • .fixed-size: Sets a fixed size of 200px width and 100px height using pixels.
  • .percentage-size: Sets the width to 50% of the parent element’s width (in this case, the parent is the .content container) and a fixed height of 80px.
  • .viewport-size: Uses viewport units: 50vw (50% of the viewport width) and 30vh (30% of the viewport height), making the element responsive to the browser window size.

2. Min-Width, Max-Width, Min-Height, Max-Height

The min-width, max-width, min-height, and max-height properties allow you to set constraints on an element’s dimensions, ensuring it doesn’t become too small or too large. These properties are especially useful for responsive design:

  • min-width and min-height: Ensure the element is at least a certain size.
  • max-width and max-height: Ensure the element doesn’t exceed a certain size.


            
Min-width and Max-width

            
Min-height and Max-height

            
            
            .min-max-width {
                width: 50%;
                min-width: 300px;
                max-width: 600px;
                height: 80px;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                border: 2px solid #333;
                text-align: center;
                line-height: 80px;
                margin: 20px 0;
            }
            .min-max-height {
                width: 200px;
                height: 150px;
                min-height: 100px;
                max-height: 200px;
                background-color: #ffe0b2;
                border: 2px solid #ff4500;
                text-align: center;
                line-height: 150px;
                margin: 20px 0;
                overflow: auto;
            }

Explanation:

  • .min-max-width: The width is 50% of the parent, but it will never be smaller than 300px (min-width) or larger than 600px (max-width).
  • .min-max-height: The height is set to 150px, but it will never be smaller than 100px (min-height) or larger than 200px (max-height). The overflow: auto is added to handle content overflow, which we’ll explore next.
These properties are great for ensuring elements remain usable and visually balanced across different screen sizes.

3. Overflow

The overflow property controls what happens when an element’s content exceeds its defined dimensions. It can be applied to both the x-axis (overflow-x) and y-axis (overflow-y), or both at once with overflow. The possible values are:

  • visible: Default. Content overflows and is visible outside the element’s boundaries.
  • hidden: Content is clipped, and the excess is hidden.
  • scroll: Adds scrollbars (both horizontal and vertical) to view the content, even if it doesn’t overflow.
  • auto: Adds scrollbars only when the content overflows.


            
Overflow: visible

            
Overflow: hidden

            
Overflow: scroll

            
Overflow: auto

            
            
            .overflow-visible, .overflow-hidden, .overflow-scroll, .overflow-auto {
                width: 200px;
                height: 100px;
                border: 2px solid #333;
                margin: 20px 0;
            }
            .overflow-visible {
                overflow: visible;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
            }
            .overflow-hidden {
                overflow: hidden;
                background-color: #ffe0b2;
            }
            .overflow-scroll {
                overflow: scroll;
                background-color: #b3e5fc;
            }
            .overflow-auto {
                overflow: auto;
                background-color: #d1c4e9;
            }
            /* Adding extra content to demonstrate overflow */
            .overflow-visible::after, .overflow-hidden::after, .overflow-scroll::after, .overflow-auto::after {
                content: "This is a long piece of text that will overflow the container. It keeps going to demonstrate how overflow works in CSS. Lorem ipsum dolor sit amet, consectetur adipiscing elit.";
                display: block;
            }

Explanation:

  • .overflow-visible: The content overflows the 200x100px box and is fully visible outside the element’s boundaries.
  • .overflow-hidden: The content is clipped, and the excess is hidden, so only the portion inside the box is visible.
  • .overflow-scroll: Scrollbars are added, allowing the user to scroll to see all the content, even if it doesn’t overflow in one direction.
  • .overflow-auto: Scrollbars are added only where needed (in this case, a vertical scrollbar because the text overflows vertically).
The overflow property is crucial for managing content in fixed-size containers, such as modals, cards, or text boxes.

Key Takeaways

  • Use width and height to set the dimensions of an element’s content area, with units like pixels, percentages, or viewport units.
  • min-width, max-width, min-height, and max-height constrain an element’s size, making layouts more flexible and responsive.
  • The overflow property manages content that exceeds an element’s dimensions, with options like visible, hidden, scroll, and auto.
  • Combine these properties to create well-structured, user-friendly layouts that handle content gracefully.

Knowledge Check

1. What does the overflow: auto property do?

Universal, Element, Class, ID, Attribute Selectors

CSS selectors are the foundation of styling web pages, allowing you to target specific HTML elements to apply styles. Selectors define which elements a set of CSS rules will apply to, and they come in various types, each with its own use case and level of specificity. In this section, we’ll explore five fundamental selectors: Universal, Element, Class, ID, and Attribute selectors. Understanding these selectors is key to writing efficient and maintainable CSS.

We’ll look at how each selector works, their syntax, and practical examples to demonstrate their usage. Let’s dive into each type of selector and see how they can be used to style HTML elements.

1. Universal Selector

The Universal Selector (*) targets all elements on a page. It’s a broad selector, often used to apply global styles or reset default browser styles (e.g., in a CSS reset). However, it should be used cautiously because it can impact performance on large pages and may unintentionally affect elements you didn’t mean to style. 


            
Div 1

            
Paragraph 1

            Span 1
            
            
            * {
                margin: 0;
                padding: 0;
                border: 1px solid #e0e0e0;
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
            }

Explanation: The universal selector * applies styles to all elements on the page. In this example, every element (div, p, span, etc.) gets a 1px light gray border and a light gray background, with margins and padding reset to 0. This is a common technique in CSS resets to ensure a consistent starting point across browsers.

2. Element Selector

The Element Selector (also called a type selector) targets all elements of a specific HTML tag name, such as p, h1, or div. It’s a simple way to apply styles to all instances of a particular element type, but it has low specificity, meaning it can be easily overridden by more specific selectors. 


            
Heading 2

            
This is a paragraph.

            
Another paragraph.

            
            
            h2 {
                color: #2e7d32;
                font-size: 24px;
            }
            p {
                font-size: 16px;
                line-height: 1.5;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
            }

Explanation:

  • h2: Targets all

    elements, setting their text color to a shade of green and font size to 24px.

  • p: Targets all

    elements, setting their font size to 16px, line height to 1.5, and background color to light cyan.

Element selectors are useful for applying consistent styles to all instances of a specific tag, but they affect every matching element on the page.

3. Class Selector

The Class Selector targets elements with a specific class attribute value. It’s denoted by a dot (.) followed by the class name (e.g., .highlight). Class selectors are more specific than element selectors and can be applied to multiple elements, making them reusable and versatile. 


            
This paragraph is highlighted.

            
This div is also highlighted.

            
This paragraph is not highlighted.

            
            
            .highlight {
                background-color: #ffeb3b;
                padding: 10px;
                border: 2px dashed #ff4500;
            }

Explanation: The class selector .highlight targets all elements with class="highlight". In this example, both the

and

elements with the highlight class get a yellow background, 10px padding, and a dashed orange border. The second paragraph, which doesn’t have the class, is unaffected.

4. ID Selector

The ID Selector targets a single element with a specific id attribute value. It’s denoted by a hash (#) followed by the ID name (e.g., #header). ID selectors are highly specific and should be used sparingly because IDs must be unique within a page. Overusing ID selectors can make CSS less maintainable. 


            
This is the header

            
This is the footer

            
            
            #header {
                background-color: #4682b4;
                color: white;
                padding: 15px;
                text-align: center;
            }
            #footer {
                background-color: #333;
                color: white;
                padding: 10px;
                text-align: center;
            }

Explanation:

  • #header: Targets the element with id="header", giving it a steel blue background, white text, 15px padding, and centered text.
  • #footer: Targets the element with id="footer", giving it a dark gray background, white text, 10px padding, and centered text.
ID selectors are useful for unique elements like headers, footers, or specific sections, but they have high specificity, which can make overriding styles more difficult.

5. Attribute Selector

The Attribute Selector targets elements based on their attributes or attribute values. It’s denoted using square brackets (e.g., [type="text"]). Attribute selectors are versatile and can match elements with a specific attribute, an attribute with a specific value, or even partial matches. Common forms include:

  • [attribute]: Matches elements with the specified attribute (e.g., [disabled]).
  • [attribute="value"]: Matches elements with the exact attribute value (e.g., [type="text"]).
  • [attribute~="value"]: Matches elements where the attribute value contains the specified word (space-separated).
  • [attribute*="value"]: Matches elements where the attribute value contains the specified substring.


            
            
            External Link
            Internal Link
            
            
            [type="text"] {
                border: 2px solid #4682b4;
                padding: 5px;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
            }
            [type="password"] {
                border: 2px solid #ff4500;
                padding: 5px;
                background-color: #ffe0b2;
            }
            [target="_blank"] {
                color: #2e7d32;
                font-weight: bold;
                text-decoration: none;
            }

Explanation:

  • [type="text"]: Targets elements with type="text", giving them a blue border, padding, and light cyan background.
  • [type="password"]: Targets elements with type="password", giving them an orange border, padding, and light orange background.
  • [target="_blank"]: Targets elements with target="_blank", making the text green, bold, and removing the underline.
Attribute selectors are powerful for styling elements based on their attributes, such as form inputs, links, or elements with specific data attributes.

Key Takeaways

  • The Universal Selector (*) targets all elements but should be used sparingly due to performance concerns.
  • Element Selectors target all elements of a specific tag (e.g., p, h1) and are good for broad styling.
  • Class Selectors (.class) are reusable and target elements with a specific class, offering moderate specificity.
  • ID Selectors (#id) target a single element with a unique ID, with high specificity, but should be used sparingly.
  • Attribute Selectors ([attribute]) target elements based on their attributes or attribute values, providing flexibility for specific use cases.

Knowledge Check

1. Which selector would you use to target an element with a specific id attribute?

Pseudo-classes (:hover, :nth-child(), :first-child, :last-child)

Pseudo-classes are special keywords in CSS that allow you to style elements based on their state, position, or user interaction, without needing to add extra classes or IDs. They are denoted by a colon (:) followed by the pseudo-class name (e.g., :hover). Pseudo-classes are incredibly useful for creating interactive and dynamic designs, such as changing an element’s appearance on hover or styling specific items in a list.

In this section, we’ll explore four commonly used pseudo-classes: :hover, :nth-child(), :first-child, and :last-child. We’ll look at their syntax, use cases, and practical examples to demonstrate how they can enhance your web designs. Let’s get started!

1. :hover Pseudo-class

The :hover pseudo-class targets an element when a user hovers over it with their mouse cursor. It’s commonly used to create interactive effects, such as changing colors, backgrounds, or other styles to provide visual feedback. Note that :hover is primarily for mouse interactions, so it may not work as expected on touch devices without additional considerations. 


            
            
Hover to change color

            
            
            .hover-button {
                background-color: #4682b4;
                color: white;
                padding: 10px 20px;
                border: none;
                cursor: pointer;
                transition: background-color 0.3s ease;
            }
            .hover-button:hover {
                background-color: #ff4500;
            }
            .hover-box {
                width: 150px;
                height: 100px;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                text-align: center;
                line-height: 100px;
                margin: 20px 0;
                transition: background-color 0.3s ease;
            }
            .hover-box:hover {
                background-color: #ffe0b2;
            }

Explanation:

  • .hover-button:hover: Changes the button’s background color from steel blue to orange-red when hovered over. The transition property ensures a smooth color change over 0.3 seconds.
  • .hover-box:hover: Changes the div’s background color from light cyan to light orange when hovered over, also with a smooth transition.
The :hover pseudo-class is great for adding interactivity, such as highlighting buttons, links, or other elements on hover.

2. :nth-child() Pseudo-class

The :nth-child() pseudo-class targets elements based on their position among a group of siblings. It accepts a formula, keyword, or number as an argument, such as :nth-child(2) to target the second child, :nth-child(even) for even-numbered children, or :nth-child(2n+1) for a custom pattern (e.g., every odd child). This pseudo-class is powerful for styling items in lists, tables, or grids. 


            
    
                
  • Item 1
    • 
                
  • Item 2
    • 
                
  • Item 3
    • 
                
  • Item 4
    • 
                
  • Item 5
    • 
            

            
            
            .nth-child-list li {
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 5px 0;
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
            }
            .nth-child-list li:nth-child(even) {
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
            }
            .nth-child-list li:nth-child(3) {
                background-color: #ffeb3b;
                font-weight: bold;
            }

Explanation:

  • .nth-child-list li: Applies base styles to all
  • elements in the list (padding, margin, and a light gray background).
  • :nth-child(even): Targets even-numbered list items (2nd, 4th, etc.) and gives them a light cyan background.
  • :nth-child(3): Targets the third list item specifically, giving it a yellow background and bold text.
The :nth-child() pseudo-class is ideal for creating patterns, such as alternating row colors in a table or highlighting specific items in a list.

3. :first-child Pseudo-class

The :first-child pseudo-class targets the first child element among a group of siblings. It’s a simpler alternative to :nth-child(1) and is often used to style the first item in a list, the first paragraph in a section, or the first element in a container. 


            

                
First paragraph

                
Second paragraph

                
Third paragraph

            

            
            
            .first-child-example p {
                margin: 10px 0;
                padding: 10px;
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
            }
            .first-child-example p:first-child {
                background-color: #4682b4;
                color: white;
                font-size: 18px;
            }

Explanation:

  • .first-child-example p: Applies base styles to all

    elements inside the container (margin, padding, and a light gray background).

  • p:first-child: Targets the first

    element among its siblings, giving it a steel blue background, white text, and a larger font size.

The :first-child pseudo-class is useful for distinguishing the first element in a group, such as the first item in a navigation menu or the first row in a table.

4. :last-child Pseudo-class

The :last-child pseudo-class targets the last child element among a group of siblings. It’s the counterpart to :first-child and is often used to style the last item in a list, the last paragraph in a section, or the last element in a container. 


            

                
First paragraph

                
Second paragraph

                
Last paragraph

            

            
            
            .last-child-example p {
                margin: 10px 0;
                padding: 10px;
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
            }
            .last-child-example p:last-child {
                background-color: #ff4500;
                color: white;
                font-style: italic;
            }

Explanation:

  • .last-child-example p: Applies base styles to all

    elements inside the container (margin, padding, and a light gray background).

  • p:last-child: Targets the last

    element among its siblings, giving it an orange-red background, white text, and italic text.

The :last-child pseudo-class is handy for styling the final element in a group, such as the last item in a list or the last button in a form.

Key Takeaways

  • :hover applies styles when a user hovers over an element, perfect for interactive effects like button highlights or link underlines.
  • :nth-child() targets elements based on their position among siblings, using numbers, keywords (e.g., even, odd), or formulas (e.g., 2n+1).
  • :first-child targets the first child element in a group, useful for styling the first item in a list or container.
  • :last-child targets the last child element in a group, ideal for styling the final item in a list or container.
  • Pseudo-classes enhance interactivity and allow for dynamic styling without modifying the HTML structure.

Knowledge Check

1. Which pseudo-class would you use to style the last element in a group of siblings?

Pseudo-elements (::before, ::after)

Pseudo-elements in CSS allow you to style specific parts of an element or insert content before or after an element’s actual content, without modifying the HTML structure. They are denoted by double colons (::), such as ::before and ::after. Pseudo-elements are powerful tools for adding decorative elements, icons, or additional styling effects, enhancing the design without cluttering the HTML.

In this section, we’ll focus on two of the most commonly used pseudo-elements: ::before and ::after. We’ll explore their syntax, how they work with the content property, and practical examples to demonstrate their usage. Let’s dive in!

1. ::before Pseudo-element

The ::before pseudo-element inserts content before the actual content of an element. It’s often used to add decorative elements, such as icons, symbols, or text, without needing to add extra HTML markup. To use ::before, you must specify the content property, which defines what to insert. The inserted content is treated as an inline element by default, but you can style it like any other element. 


            
Heading with Icon

            
This paragraph has a decorative line.

            
            
            .before-heading::before {
                content: "★ ";
                color: #ff4500;
                margin-right: 5px;
            }
            .before-text::before {
                content: "";
                display: inline-block;
                width: 10px;
                height: 10px;
                background-color: #4682b4;
                margin-right: 10px;
            }
            .before-heading, .before-text {
                margin: 20px 0;
                padding: 10px;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
            }

Explanation:

  • .before-heading::before: Inserts a star symbol (★) before the heading’s text, colored orange-red, with a small margin to the right for spacing.
  • .before-text::before: Inserts an empty content value but creates a 10x10px blue square (using display: inline-block) as a decorative element before the paragraph, with a margin to the right.
The ::before pseudo-element is great for adding visual flair, such as icons, bullets, or decorative shapes, without modifying the HTML.

2. ::after Pseudo-element

The ::after pseudo-element inserts content after the actual content of an element. Like ::before, it requires the content property and is often used for decorative purposes, such as adding a suffix, an arrow, or a visual effect. The inserted content is also inline by default but can be styled as needed. 


            
Heading with Suffix

            
This paragraph has an arrow.

            
            
            .after-heading::after {
                content: " ✔";
                color: #2e7d32;
                margin-left: 5px;
            }
            .after-text::after {
                content: " →";
                color: #ff4500;
                font-weight: bold;
                margin-left: 10px;
            }
            .after-heading, .after-text {
                margin: 20px 0;
                padding: 10px;
                background-color: #ffe0b2;
            }

Explanation:

  • .after-heading::after: Inserts a green checkmark (✔) after the heading’s text, with a small margin to the left for spacing.
  • .after-text::after: Inserts an orange arrow (→) after the paragraph’s text, styled as bold with a margin to the left.
The ::after pseudo-element is useful for adding suffixes, indicators, or decorative elements at the end of an element’s content.

3. Using ::before and ::after Together

You can use ::before and ::after together on the same element to add content both before and after the element’s actual content. This is often used for creating complex decorative effects, such as quotation marks around a quote, badges, or custom list markers. 


            
This is a famous quote.

            
            
            .quote {
                font-style: italic;
                padding: 20px;
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
                border-left: 5px solid #4682b4;
                margin: 20px 0;
                position: relative;
            }
            .quote::before {
                content: open-quote;
                font-size: 30px;
                color: #ff4500;
                margin-right: 10px;
            }
            .quote::after {
                content: close-quote;
                font-size: 30px;
                color: #ff4500;
                margin-left: 10px;
            }

Explanation:

  • .quote: Styles the blockquote with italic text, padding, a light gray background, and a blue left border.
  • .quote::before: Adds an opening quotation mark (open-quote) before the quote, styled in orange and larger font size.
  • .quote::after: Adds a closing quotation mark (close-quote) after the quote, also in orange and larger font size.
Using ::before and ::after together allows you to create visually appealing effects, like wrapping a quote in quotation marks, without adding extra HTML elements.

Key Takeaways

  • ::before inserts content before an element’s actual content, requiring the content property, and is great for adding icons, symbols, or decorative elements.
  • ::after inserts content after an element’s actual content, also requiring the content property, and is useful for suffixes, arrows, or visual indicators.
  • Both pseudo-elements can be styled like regular elements (e.g., with colors, sizes, or positioning) and are inline by default unless specified otherwise.
  • Using ::before and ::after together allows for creative designs, such as adding quotation marks, badges, or custom list markers, without modifying the HTML.

Knowledge Check

1. What property must you use with ::before and ::after to insert content?
