    Floats & Clearing Floats

    The float property in CSS is a traditional method for positioning elements, originally designed to allow text to wrap around images (like in a magazine layout). It removes elements from the normal document flow, causing them to "float" to the left or right of their container, with other content wrapping around them. However, floats can lead to layout issues, such as parent containers collapsing, which is where clearing techniques come in.

    In this section, we’ll explore how to use the float property to create layouts, the problems that arise from floating elements, and how to address them using the clear property and modern clearing methods like the clearfix technique. We’ll provide practical examples to demonstrate their usage in real-world scenarios, such as creating a two-column layout or an image-text wrap. Let’s get started!

    1. Using the Float Property

    The float property removes an element from the normal document flow and pushes it to the left or right of its container, allowing inline content (like text) to wrap around it. Common values include:

    • left: Floats the element to the left, with content wrapping on the right.
    • right: Floats the element to the right, with content wrapping on the left.
    • none: The default, meaning the element does not float.
    Floated elements are still part of the document flow in the sense that they affect the layout of surrounding content, but they can cause issues with parent containers. 

    
            
    
                
                
    This text wraps around the floated image. Floats are often used to create layouts where content flows around an element, such as an image in a magazine-style article. Notice how the text continues to wrap until it clears the image.
    
            
    
            
            
            .float-container {
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #ff4500;
            }
            .float-left {
                float: left;
                margin-right: 15px;
            }

    Explanation:

    • The .float-left image is floated to the left, causing the paragraph text to wrap around it on the right.
    • The margin-right: 15px adds space between the image and the wrapping text for better readability.
    Use float to create layouts where content needs to wrap around an element, such as images in articles or icons in a list.

    2. Floating Multiple Elements

    You can float multiple elements to create layouts like columns. Floated elements will align side by side as long as there’s enough horizontal space in the parent container. However, this can cause the parent container to collapse (not account for the height of the floated children), which we’ll address in the next section. 

    
            
    
                
    Column 1
    
                
    Column 2
    
                
    Column 3
    
            
    
            
            
            .float-columns {
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
            }
            .column {
                float: left;
                width: 30%;
                margin: 1.66%; /* (100% - 3 * 30%) / 6 for equal spacing */
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 10px;
                border: 1px solid #2e7d32;
                text-align: center;
            }

    Explanation:

    • Each .column div is floated to the left with a width of 30%, creating a three-column layout.
    • The margin: 1.66% ensures equal spacing between columns (calculated as the remaining space divided by the number of gaps).
    • The parent container .float-columns may collapse (appear to have no height) because its children are floated, which we’ll fix later.
    Floating multiple elements is a traditional way to create grid-like layouts, though modern methods like Flexbox or CSS Grid are often preferred today.

    3. The Clear Property

    The clear property is used to control the behavior of elements that follow floated elements. It ensures that an element does not wrap around a floated element by forcing it to start on a new line below the floated content. Common values include:

    • left: Clears elements floated to the left.
    • right: Clears elements floated to the right.
    • both: Clears elements floated to either side.
    • none: The default, allowing wrapping around floated elements.

    
            
    
                
                
    This text wraps around the image.
    
                
    This text clears the float and starts on a new line below the image.
    
            
    
            
            
            .float-container {
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #ff4500;
            }
            .float-left {
                float: left;
                margin-right: 15px;
            }
            .clear-example {
                clear: left;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 10px;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
            }

    Explanation:

    • The first paragraph wraps around the floated image.
    • The .clear-example paragraph uses clear: left to force it to start on a new line below the floated image, preventing wrapping.
    Use clear to ensure elements following floated content start on a new line, avoiding unwanted wrapping.

    4. Clearing Floats with the Clearfix Technique

    When all children of a container are floated, the parent container collapses (its height becomes 0) because floated elements are removed from the normal flow. The clear property can be used to fix this by adding a clearing element, but a more modern and cleaner approach is the "clearfix" technique. This involves adding a pseudo-element to the parent container to clear the floats, ensuring the parent wraps around its floated children. 

    
            
    
                
    Column 1
    
                
    Column 2
    
                
    Column 3
    
            
    
            
    Content after the floated columns.
    
            
            
            .float-columns {
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
            }
            .column {
                float: left;
                width: 30%;
                margin: 1.66%;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 10px;
                border: 1px solid #2e7d32;
                text-align: center;
            }
            .clearfix::after {
                content: "";
                display: block;
                clear: both;
            }
            .content-after {
                background-color: #f5f5f5;
                padding: 10px;
                border: 1px solid #ff4500;
            }

    Explanation:

    • The .float-columns container has the clearfix class, which uses the ::after pseudo-element to add an invisible block element that clears both sides (clear: both).
    • This ensures the parent container wraps around the floated columns, giving it the correct height and preventing the .content-after div from moving up into the floated content.
    The clearfix technique is a widely used method to handle float clearing without adding extra markup, making it a clean solution for float-based layouts.

    5. Practical Use Case: Image Gallery with Floats

    Let’s see how float and clearing techniques can be used in a real-world scenario, such as creating an image gallery where images are floated to form a grid, and the container properly wraps around them using the clearfix method. 

    
            
    
                
                
                
                
            
    
            
    Content after the gallery.
    
            
            
            .gallery {
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
            }
            .gallery-item {
                float: left;
                width: 150px;
                margin: 5px;
            }
            .clearfix::after {
                content: "";
                display: block;
                clear: both;
            }
            .content-after {
                background-color: #f5f5f5;
                padding: 10px;
                border: 1px solid #ff4500;
            }

    Explanation:

    • Each .gallery-item image is floated to the left with a fixed width of 150px, creating a grid-like layout as the images wrap within the container.
    • The .gallery container uses the clearfix class to ensure it wraps around the floated images, maintaining its height.
    • The .content-after div starts on a new line below the gallery, thanks to the clearfix.
    This example demonstrates how to use floats to create a simple image gallery, with proper clearing to maintain the layout.

    Key Takeaways

    • The float property removes elements from the normal flow, pushing them to the left or right, with content wrapping around them.
    • Floating multiple elements can create layouts like columns, but it may cause the parent container to collapse.
    • The clear property prevents elements from wrapping around floated content, forcing them to start on a new line.
    • The clearfix technique uses a pseudo-element to clear floats, ensuring parent containers wrap around floated children without extra markup.

    Knowledge Check

    1. What does the clear: both property do?





    Previous Next

    CSS Units (px, em, rem, %, vh, vw, fr)

    CSS units are used to define the size of elements, spacing, and other measurements in a webpage layout. Different units serve different purposes, offering flexibility for creating responsive and scalable designs. Some units are absolute (fixed in size), while others are relative (dependent on context, such as parent elements, root elements, or the viewport). The most commonly used CSS units include px, em, rem, %, vh, vw, and fr.

    In this section, we’ll explore these CSS units, their characteristics, and how they can be applied in various scenarios. We’ll provide practical examples to demonstrate their usage, from fixed pixel-based layouts to responsive designs that adapt to the viewport or parent elements. Let’s dive in!

    1. Pixels (px)

    The px unit is an absolute unit that represents a fixed size in pixels. It’s the most straightforward unit, providing precise control over element dimensions. Pixels are not affected by the context of the element (e.g., parent or root font sizes) and are commonly used for fixed-size elements like borders, icons, or small UI components. 

    
            
    Box with 200px width and 100px height
    
            
            
            .px-example {
                width: 200px;
                height: 100px;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
                text-align: center;
            }

    Explanation:

    • The .px-example div has a fixed width of 200px and height of 100px, regardless of the screen size or parent context.
    • Pixels provide consistent sizing across devices, but they don’t scale with the user’s browser settings (e.g., font size adjustments).
    Use px for elements that need a fixed, predictable size, such as icons, borders, or small UI components.

    2. Em (em)

    The em unit is a relative unit that is based on the font size of the element itself or its nearest parent with a defined font size. If the element’s font size is 16px, then 1em equals 16px for that element. This makes em a scalable unit, but it can lead to compounding effects when nested elements inherit font sizes. 

    
            
    
                
    Text with 2em font size
    
                
    Nested text with 1.5em font size
    
            
    
            
            
            .em-container {
                font-size: 16px; /* Base font size */
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #ff4500;
            }
            .em-example {
                font-size: 2em; /* 2 * 16px = 32px */
            }
            .em-nested {
                font-size: 1.5em; /* 1.5 * 32px = 48px (compounding effect) */
                color: #2e7d32;
            }

    Explanation:

    • The .em-container has a font size of 16px, so .em-example with font-size: 2em becomes 32px (2 * 16px).
    • The .em-nested element inherits the 32px font size from its parent and applies 1.5em, resulting in 48px (1.5 * 32px), demonstrating the compounding effect.
    Use em for sizes that should scale relative to the element’s or parent’s font size, such as padding or margins that need to adjust with text size.

    3. Rem (rem)

    The rem unit is also a relative unit, but it is based on the font size of the root element (usually the element), not the parent. By default, the root font size is 16px in most browsers (unless changed by the user or CSS). This makes rem more predictable than em because it avoids the compounding effect of nested elements. 

    
            
    
                
    Text with 2rem font size
    
                
    Nested text with 1.5rem font size
    
            
    
            
            
            html {
                font-size: 16px; /* Root font size */
            }
            .rem-container {
                font-size: 20px; /* Does not affect rem */
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #ff4500;
            }
            .rem-example {
                font-size: 2rem; /* 2 * 16px = 32px (based on root) */
            }
            .rem-nested {
                font-size: 1.5rem; /* 1.5 * 16px = 24px (based on root) */
                color: #2e7d32;
            }

    Explanation:

    • The root font size is 16px, so .rem-example with font-size: 2rem becomes 32px (2 * 16px).
    • The .rem-nested element uses 1.5rem, which is 24px (1.5 * 16px), based on the root font size, not the parent’s 20px font size, avoiding compounding.
    Use rem for consistent scaling across the entire page, such as font sizes or spacing that should be relative to the root font size.

    4. Percentage (%)

    The % unit is a relative unit that is based on the size of the parent element. For example, a width of 50% means the element will take up 50% of the parent’s width. Percentages are commonly used for responsive layouts where elements need to scale proportionally with their container. 

    
            
    
                
    50% width of parent
    
            
    
            
            
            .percent-container {
                width: 400px;
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #ff4500;
            }
            .percent-example {
                width: 50%; /* 50% of 400px = 200px */
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 10px;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
                text-align: center;
            }

    Explanation:

    • The .percent-container has a width of 400px, so the .percent-example with width: 50% becomes 200px (50% of 400px).
    • If the parent’s width changes (e.g., due to a responsive design), the percentage-based width will adjust automatically.
    Use % for responsive designs where elements need to scale proportionally with their parent, such as columns or containers.

    5. Viewport Units (vh, vw)

    The vh and vw units are relative to the viewport’s dimensions. vh stands for "viewport height," where 1vh is 1% of the viewport’s height, and vw stands for "viewport width," where 1vw is 1% of the viewport’s width. These units are ideal for creating layouts that scale with the browser window. 

    
            
    Full viewport height section
    
            
            
            .viewport-example {
                height: 50vh; /* 50% of viewport height */
                width: 50vw; /* 50% of viewport width */
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 20px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
                text-align: center;
            }

    Explanation:

    • The .viewport-example div takes up 50% of the viewport’s height (50vh) and 50% of the viewport’s width (50vw).
    • If the browser window is resized, the div’s dimensions will adjust automatically to maintain the 50% proportion.
    Use vh and vw for elements that need to scale with the viewport, such as full-screen sections, hero images, or responsive typography.

    6. Fraction (fr)

    The fr unit is a relative unit used specifically with CSS Grid to represent a fraction of the available space in a grid container. It allows you to distribute space proportionally among grid tracks (rows or columns). For example, 1fr takes up one fraction of the remaining space after fixed sizes are accounted for. 

    
            
    
                
    Item 1
    
                
    Item 2
    
                
    Item 3
    
            
    
            
            
            .grid-container {
                display: grid;
                grid-template-columns: 1fr 2fr 1fr; /* 1:2:1 ratio */
                grid-gap: 10px;
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #ff4500;
            }
            .grid-item {
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 10px;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
                text-align: center;
            }

    Explanation:

    • The grid-template-columns: 1fr 2fr 1fr creates three columns in a 1:2:1 ratio. The middle column takes up twice as much space as the other two.
    • The fr unit distributes the available space after accounting for fixed sizes (like the 10px gaps).
    Use fr with CSS Grid to create flexible, proportional layouts for grid tracks, such as columns or rows in a dashboard or gallery.

    7. Practical Use Case: Responsive Hero Section

    Let’s see how different CSS units can be combined to create a responsive hero section that adapts to the viewport, scales typography, and ensures consistent spacing. 

    
            
    
                
    Welcome to Our Site
    
                
    This is a responsive hero section using various CSS units.
    
            
    
            
            
            .hero-section {
                height: 50vh; /* 50% of viewport height */
                width: 100%; /* Full width of parent */
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 2rem; /* 2 * root font size */
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
                text-align: center;
            }
            .hero-title {
                font-size: 3rem; /* 3 * root font size */
                margin-bottom: 1em; /* 1 * element's font size */
            }
            .hero-text {
                font-size: 1.5rem; /* 1.5 * root font size */
                max-width: 80%; /* 80% of parent width */
                margin: 0 auto;
            }

    Explanation:

    • height: 50vh: The hero section takes up 50% of the viewport height.
    • width: 100%: The section spans the full width of its parent.
    • padding: 2rem: Padding scales with the root font size (e.g., 32px if the root is 16px).
    • font-size: 3rem and 1.5rem: Typography scales consistently with the root font size.
    • margin-bottom: 1em: The margin scales with the element’s font size (e.g., 48px for the title if its font size is 48px).
    • max-width: 80%: The text block takes up 80% of the parent’s width, ensuring readability.
    This example demonstrates how to combine multiple CSS units to create a responsive, scalable hero section.

    Key Takeaways

    • px is an absolute unit for fixed sizes, ideal for elements that don’t need to scale.
    • em is relative to the element’s or parent’s font size, useful for scaling within a context but can compound in nested elements.
    • rem is relative to the root font size, providing consistent scaling without compounding effects.
    • % is relative to the parent’s size, perfect for proportional layouts in responsive designs.
    • vh and vw are relative to the viewport’s height and width, ideal for viewport-based layouts.
    • fr is used with CSS Grid to distribute space proportionally among grid tracks.

    Knowledge Check

    1. What does the rem unit base its size on?





    Previous Next

    Media Queries (Responsive Design)

    Media queries are a cornerstone of responsive web design, allowing you to apply CSS styles based on the characteristics of the device or viewport, such as its width, height, orientation, or resolution. They enable you to create layouts that adapt seamlessly to different screen sizes, from mobile phones to desktops, ensuring a consistent user experience across devices.

    In this section, we’ll explore the syntax of media queries, common use cases, and best practices for responsive design. We’ll provide practical examples to demonstrate how to use media queries to adjust layouts, typography, and other styles based on screen size. Let’s get started!

    1. Media Query Basics

    A media query consists of a media type (e.g., screen, print) and one or more conditions (e.g., min-width, max-width). If the conditions are met, the styles inside the media query are applied. The basic syntax is: 

    @media (condition) {
                /* Styles */
            }
    Common conditions include:
    • min-width: Applies styles if the viewport width is greater than or equal to the specified value.
    • max-width: Applies styles if the viewport width is less than or equal to the specified value.
    • orientation: Applies styles based on the device orientation (portrait or landscape).

    
            
    Resize the window to see me change!
    
            
            
            .media-query-box {
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 20px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
                text-align: center;
                font-size: 16px;
            }
            /* Small screens (up to 600px) */
            @media (max-width: 600px) {
                .media-query-box {
                    background-color: #ff4500;
                    font-size: 14px;
                }
            }
            /* Medium screens (601px to 900px) */
            @media (min-width: 601px) and (max-width: 900px) {
                .media-query-box {
                    background-color: #2e7d32;
                    font-size: 18px;
                }
            }
            /* Large screens (901px and above) */
            @media (min-width: 901px) {
                .media-query-box {
                    background-color: #4682b4;
                    font-size: 20px;
                }
            }

    Explanation:

    • The .media-query-box has default styles for all screen sizes.
    • @media (max-width: 600px): On small screens (up to 600px), the box turns orange-red and the font size decreases to 14px.
    • @media (min-width: 601px) and (max-width: 900px): On medium screens (601px to 900px), the box turns green and the font size increases to 18px.
    • @media (min-width: 901px): On large screens (901px and above), the box turns blue and the font size increases to 20px.
    Media queries allow you to tailor styles to specific screen sizes, creating a responsive experience.

    2. Common Breakpoints

    Breakpoints are the points at which your design changes to accommodate different screen sizes. While breakpoints can be customized based on your design needs, common ones include:

    • Small screens (mobile): Up to 600px (max-width: 600px)
    • Medium screens (tablets): 601px to 900px (min-width: 601px and max-width: 900px)
    • Large screens (desktops): 901px and above (min-width: 901px)
    These breakpoints are just a starting point; you should adjust them based on your content and design requirements. 

    
            
    
                
    Item 1
    
                
    Item 2
    
                
    Item 3
    
            
    
            
            
            .breakpoint-example {
                display: flex;
                flex-wrap: wrap;
                gap: 10px;
                background-color: #f0f0f0;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #ff4500;
            }
            .breakpoint-item {
                flex: 1 1 100%; /* Full width on small screens */
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 10px;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
                text-align: center;
            }
            /* Medium screens: Two items per row */
            @media (min-width: 601px) {
                .breakpoint-item {
                    flex: 1 1 calc(50% - 5px); /* Two items per row */
                }
            }
            /* Large screens: Three items per row */
            @media (min-width: 901px) {
                .breakpoint-item {
                    flex: 1 1 calc(33.33% - 7px); /* Three items per row */
                }
            }

    Explanation:

    • On small screens (up to 600px), each .breakpoint-item takes up 100% of the container width, stacking vertically.
    • On medium screens (601px and above), items take up 50% of the width (minus half the gap), creating a two-column layout.
    • On large screens (901px and above), items take up 33.33% of the width (minus a third of the gap), creating a three-column layout.
    Using breakpoints, you can adjust layouts to optimize for different devices, ensuring usability and readability.

    3. Orientation and Other Conditions

    Media queries can also target conditions beyond screen width, such as the device’s orientation or resolution. For example, you can apply different styles for portrait and landscape orientations, which is particularly useful for mobile devices. 

    
            
    Rotate your device or resize the window to see me change!
    
            
            
            .orientation-example {
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
                padding: 20px;
                margin: 10px 0;
                border: 1px solid #4682b4;
                text-align: center;
                font-size: 16px;
            }
            /* Portrait orientation */
            @media (orientation: portrait) {
                .orientation-example {
                    background-color: #ff4500;
                    font-size: 14px;
                }
            }
            /* Landscape orientation */
            @media (orientation: landscape) {
                .orientation-example {
                    background-color: #2e7d32;
                    font-size: 18px;
                }
            }

    Explanation:

    • In portrait orientation (taller than wide), the .orientation-example div turns orange-red with a smaller font size.
    • In landscape orientation (wider than tall), the div turns green with a larger font size.
    Use orientation-based media queries to optimize layouts for mobile devices, such as adjusting font sizes or rearranging elements for better usability.

    4. Practical Use Case: Responsive Navigation Menu

    Let’s see how media queries can be used in a real-world scenario to create a responsive navigation menu that switches between a hamburger menu on small screens and a horizontal menu on larger screens. 

    
            
    
                
    ☰
    
                
      
                    
    • Home
      • 
                    
    • About
      • 
                    
    • Services
      • 
                    
    • Contact
      • 
                
    
            
    
            
            
            .responsive-nav {
                background-color: #4682b4;
                padding: 10px;
                margin: 10px 0;
            }
            .hamburger {
                display: block;
                font-size: 24px;
                color: white;
                cursor: pointer;
            }
            .nav-links {
                display: none; /* Hidden by default on small screens */
                list-style: none;
                padding: 0;
                margin: 0;
                background-color: #e0f7fa;
            }
            .nav-links li {
                padding: 10px;
            }
            .nav-links a {
                color: #4682b4;
                text-decoration: none;
                font-weight: 700;
            }
            /* Medium screens and above: Show horizontal menu */
            @media (min-width: 601px) {
                .hamburger {
                    display: none; /* Hide hamburger on larger screens */
                }
                .nav-links {
                    display: flex; /* Show as horizontal menu */
                    background-color: transparent;
                }
                .nav-links li {
                    margin: 0 15px;
                }
                .nav-links a {
                    color: white;
                }
            }

    Explanation:

    • On small screens (up to 600px), the navigation links are hidden, and a hamburger icon is displayed, simulating a mobile menu (you’d typically add JavaScript to toggle the menu).
    • On medium screens and above (601px and up), the hamburger icon is hidden, and the navigation links are displayed as a horizontal menu using Flexbox.
    This example demonstrates how media queries can create a responsive navigation menu that adapts to different screen sizes, improving usability.

    Key Takeaways

    • Media queries allow you to apply styles based on device characteristics like viewport width, height, orientation, or resolution.
    • Use min-width and max-width to define breakpoints for different screen sizes, such as mobile, tablet, and desktop.
    • Orientation-based media queries (orientation: portrait or landscape) help optimize layouts for mobile devices.
    • Media queries are essential for responsive design, enabling layouts, typography, and other styles to adapt to the user’s device.

    Knowledge Check

    1. What does the media query @media (max-width: 600px) do?
